                10.1007/bf00658014</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D1%26publication_year%3D1983%26pages%3D45-51%26journal%3DJ.%2BIncl.%2BPhenom.%26author%3DA.%2BMaazaz%26author%3DC.%2BDelmas%26author%3DP.%2BHagenmuller%26title%3DA%2Bstudy%2Bof%2Bthe%2BNaxTiO2%2Bsystem%2Bby%2Belectrochemical%2Bdeintercalation%26doi%3D10.1007%252Fbf00658014&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.8b11609&amp;doiOfLink=10.1007%2Fbf00658014&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit47R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">47</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A study of the sodium titanium oxide (NaxTiO2) system by electrochemical deintercalation</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Maazaz, A.; Delmas, C.; Hagenmuller, P.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Inclusion Phenomena</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">1983</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">1</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">45-51</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JOIPDF</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0167-7861</span>.
    </div><div class="casAbstract">The electrochem. deintercalation from the sheet oxide NaTiO2 leads to new NaxTiO2 materials.  The deintercalation is reversible over the range 0.70 .ltorsim. x ≤ 1, whereas irreversible modification of the host structure occur by overvoltage (x .ltorsim. 0.70).  This behavior, which can be explained by migration of some Ti ions from the slabs to the intersheet space, may be considered as a 2-dimensional → 3-dimensional structural transition.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGokeg4GiB6gBrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lj96pg20eY5BQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADyaL3sXls1Gltbc%253D&amp;md5=bd2b4401a87d4bbf694524aba89fbb5b</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref48"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref48'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">48</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit48" data-doi="10.1039/c5cp06179b"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lu, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Song, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Pan, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cheng, Y.-T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Voltage Hysteresis of Lithium Ion Batteries Caused by Mechanical Stress</span>. <i>Phys. Room temperature Co 2p photoelectron spectra of the P3- and O3-forms of Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Co<sub>0.5</sub>Ti<sub>0.5</sub>O<sub>2</sub> together with the Co<sup>2+</sup> reference material, CoO. The energy difference between Co 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and Co 2p<sub>1/2</sub> of about 15.3 eV and between Co 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and the satellite peak of 6.2 eV is typical for oxides with Co<sup>2+</sup>, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref30 ref31'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30 ref31" aria-label="reference 30 and 31">(30,31)</a> whereas the energy difference is 10 eV between Co 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and satellite peaks for Co<sup>3+</sup>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref28'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28" aria-label="reference 28">(28)</a></p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b11609/asset/images/large/am-2018-11609g_0009.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b11609&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure data-id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><a class="internalNav" aria-label="scroll to figure" href="#fig4"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b11609/asset/images/medium/am-2018-11609g_0010.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b11609/asset/images/large/am-2018-11609g_0010.jpeg" id="rightTab-gr4" class="rightTab-fig internalNav"></a><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. (a) Temperature evolution of NPD patterns of O3-Na<sub>0.85</sub>Co<sub>0.5</sub>Ti<sub>0.5</sub>O<sub>2</sub>. Above 550 °C, reflections of the P3-phase appear in the 2θ region between 40° and 80°, reflecting the phase transformation O3 → P3. (b). Temperature dependence of cell parameters and average (Co,Ti)–O interatomic distances for O3- and P3-Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Co<sub>0.5</sub>Ti<sub>0.5</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, from NPD and synchrotron powder diffraction experiments upon heating. The new P3-Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Co<sub>0.5</sub>Ti<sub>0.5</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, marked with red triangles, arises from O3-Na<sub>0.85</sub>Co<sub>0.5</sub>Ti<sub>0.5</sub>O<sub>2</sub> above 550 °C.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b11609/asset/images/large/am-2018-11609g_0010.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b11609&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure data-id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><a class="internalNav" aria-label="scroll to figure" href="#fig5"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b11609/asset/images/medium/am-2018-11609g_0011.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b11609/asset/images/large/am-2018-11609g_0011.jpeg" id="rightTab-gr5" class="rightTab-fig internalNav"></a><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. Temperature dependence of the first charging–discharging cycle for (a) P3 (<i>R</i>3<i>m</i>) and (b) O3 (<i>R</i>3̅<i>m</i>) modifications of Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Co<sub>0.5</sub>Ti<sub>0.5</sub>O<sub>2</sub>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b11609/asset/images/large/am-2018-11609g_0011.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b11609&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure data-id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><a class="internalNav" aria-label="scroll to figure" href="#fig6"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b11609/asset/images/medium/am-2018-11609g_0012.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b11609/asset/images/large/am-2018-11609g_0012.jpeg" id="rightTab-gr6" class="rightTab-fig internalNav"></a><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. Galvanostatic cycling of the O3- and P3-phases for the first and fifth cycles in the potential window 4.2–0.2 V vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na. For the initial P3-phase, a “constant voltage” mode was additionally applied at the end of cell discharging until the residual current reached 10% of the initial value.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b11609/asset/images/large/am-2018-11609g_0012.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b11609&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure data-id="fig7" data-index="7" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 7</h2><a class="internalNav" aria-label="scroll to figure" href="#fig7"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b11609/asset/images/medium/am-2018-11609g_0013.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b11609/asset/images/large/am-2018-11609g_0013.jpeg" id="rightTab-gr7" class="rightTab-fig internalNav"></a><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 7. Chemical diffusion coefficients of Na<sup>+</sup> in (a) P3-Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Co<sub>0.5</sub>Ti<sub>0.5</sub>O<sub>2</sub> and (b) O3-Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Co<sub>0.5</sub>Ti<sub>0.5</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, obtained by GITT measurements during the first charging–discharging cycle.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b11609/asset/images/large/am-2018-11609g_0013.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b11609&amp;id=fig7"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure data-id="fig8" data-index="8" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 8</h2><a class="internalNav" aria-label="scroll to figure" href="#fig8"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b11609/asset/images/medium/am-2018-11609g_0014.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b11609/asset/images/large/am-2018-11609g_0014.jpeg" id="rightTab-gr8" class="rightTab-fig internalNav"></a><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 8. Part of the operando synchrotron powder diffraction data as contour plots from (a) O3-Na<sub>0.95</sub>Co<sub>0.5</sub>Ti<sub>0.5</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (<i>R</i>3̅<i>m</i>) and (b) P3-Na<sub>0.65</sub>Co<sub>0.5</sub>Ti<sub>0.5</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (<i>R</i>3<i>m</i>) as cathodes in electrochemical cells with the Na anode during galvanostatic cycling at C/20 (right) and voltage profile of the in situ cells vs time (left). Upon sodiation of both materials down to 1.2 V, the P3 structure is stable, whereas a decrease of the cell voltage below 1 V is accompanied by the formation of the O3 structure.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The status of ambient temp. sodium ion batteries is reviewed in light of recent developments in anode, electrolyte and cathode materials.  These devices, although early in their stage of development, are promising for large-scale grid storage applications due to the abundance and very low cost of sodium-contg. precursors used to make the components.  The engineering knowledge developed recently for highly successful Li ion batteries can be leveraged to ensure rapid progress in this area, although different electrode materials and electrolytes will be required for dual intercalation systems based on sodium.  In particular, new anode materials need to be identified, since the graphite anode, commonly used in lithium systems, does not intercalate sodium to any appreciable extent.  A wider array of choices is available for cathodes, including high performance layered transition metal oxides and polyanionic compds.  Recent developments in electrodes are encouraging, but a great deal of research is necessary, particularly in new electrolytes, and the understanding of the SEI films.  The engineering modeling calcns. of Na-ion battery energy d. indicate that 210 Wh kg-1 in gravimetric energy is possible for Na-ion batteries compared to existing Li-ion technol. if a cathode capacity of 200 mAh g-1 and a 500 mAh g-1 anode can be discovered with an av. cell potential of 3.3 V.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGojsFXLPEQzILVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0li41NOdIt3oTg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38XntlSnurc%253D&amp;md5=fa5d7d089d261a40896cb6b03e88bfbe</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref2" class="rightTabRefNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit2" data-doi="10.1039/c4ee03192j"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Han, M.                         rightTab-cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A comprehensive review of sodium layered oxides: powerful cathodes for Na-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Han, Man Huon; Gonzalo, Elena; Singh, Gurpreet; Rojo, Teofilo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The room temp. Na-ion secondary battery has been under focus lately due to its feasibility to compete against the already well-established Li-ion secondary battery.  Although there are many obstacles to overcome before the Na-ion battery becomes com. available, recent research discoveries corroborate that some of the cathode materials for the Na-ion battery have indeed indisputable advantages over its Li-ion counterparts.  In this publication, a comprehensive review of layered oxides (NaTMO2, TM = Ti, V, Cr, Mn, Fe, Co, Ni, and a mixt. of 2 or 3 elements) as a viable Na-ion battery cathode is presented.  Single TM systems are well characterized not only for their electrochem. performance but also for their structural transitions during the cycle.  Binary TM systems are investigated in order to address issues regarding low reversible capacity, capacity retention, operating voltage, and structural stability.  As a consequence, some materials already have reached an energy d. of 520 mW h g-1, which is comparable to that of LiFePO4.  Furthermore, some ternary TM systems retained more than 72% of their capacity along with over 99.7% Coulombic efficiency for 275 cycles.  The goal of this review is to present the development of Na layered oxide materials in the past as well as the state of the art today in order to emphasize the compatibility and durability of layered oxides as powerful candidates for Na-ion battery cathode materials.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpwo96Ash7WNbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljt_Qt3lqLyzw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXitVCrsLjE&amp;md5=d938be66a2a0948e3d2f589b803dbf76</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref3" class="rightTabRefNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit3" data-doi="10.1016/0167-2738(81)90076-x"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Delmas, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Braconnier, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fouassier, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hagenmuller, P.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Electrochemical intercalation of sodium in Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>CoO<sub>2</sub> bronzes</span>. <i>Solid State Ionics</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">1981</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>3-4</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">165</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">169</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1016/0167-2738(81)90076-x</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrochemical intercalation of sodium in sodium cobalt oxide (NaxCoO2) bronzes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Delmas, Claude; Braconnier, Jean Jacques; Fouassier, Claude; Hagenmuller, Paul</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Solid State Ionics</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">1981</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">3-4</span>

    </div><div class="casAbstract">The chem. study of the NaxCoO2 system (0.5 ≤ x ≤1) has shown previously the existence of bronze-type phases with layer structure.  In each material, the lattice is built up by sheets of edge-sharing CoO6 octahedra allowing Na+ to be intercalated with trigonal prismatic surrounding (AABBCC or AABB packing of O) for small values of x and octahedral environment (ABCABC packing of O) for larger ones.  Discharge potentials (2.0 &lt; V &lt; 3.5 V) were measured as a function of the compn.  The electrochem. intercalation of Na in all these materials is reversible within their existence range.  During the electrochem. intercalation, a reversible charge is obsd. between phases having AABBCC and ABCABC packing of O.  This behavior can be explained by the relatively small displacement of the sheets.  In contrast, for the material with AABB packing of O, no transition occurs because a packing change requires breaking of Co-O bonds, which is apparently impossible at room temp.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrkktugvcleXLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljt_Qt3lqLyzw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADyaL3MXlvFChtL8%253D&amp;md5=3f450557c36835cd364b52b72a333f93</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref4" class="rightTabRefNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit4" data-doi="10.1103/physrevlett.92.247001"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Foo, M.         </div><div class="casAbstract">We measured and analyzed the transport properties of single-crystal NaCo2O4, which is a metallic transition-metal oxide consisting of a two-dimensional triangle lattice of Co.  Reflecting the crystal structure, the resistivity is highly anisotropic between in- and out-of-plane directions, and the in-plane resistivity is as low as 200 μΩ cm at 300 K. Most strikingly, the in-plane thermoelec. power of NaCo2O4 is about 100 μV/K at 300 K, which is nearly ten times larger than that of typical metals.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Research on the oxide perovskites has uncovered electronic properties that are strikingly enhanced compared with those in conventional metals.  Examples are the high crit. temps. of the cuprate superconductors and the colossal magnetoresistance in the manganites.  The conducting layered cobaltate NaxCoO2 exhibits several interesting electronic phases as the Na content x is varied, including water-induced supercond. and an insulating state that is destroyed by field.  Initial measurements showed that, in the as-grown compn., NaxCoO2 has moderately large thermopower S and cond. σ.  However, the prospects for thermoelec. cooling applications faded when the figure of merit Z was found to be small at this compn. (0.6&lt;x&lt;0.7).  Here we report that, in the poorly explored high-doping region x&gt;0.75, S undergoes an even steeper enhancement.  At the crit. doping xp∼0.85, Z (at 80 K) reaches values ∼40 times larger than in the as-grown crystals.  We discuss prospects for low-temp. thermoelec. applications.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGplMQADzYFC-7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljt_Qt3lqLyzw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD28XmsVGjsbo%253D&amp;md5=a9a12b6e2282c2f10c9c9fd4507b4dc9</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref7"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref7" class="rightTabRefNumLink">7</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit7" data-doi="10.1038/nature01450"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Takada, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sakurai, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Takayama-Muromachi, E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Izumi, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dilanian, R.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Since the discovery of high-transition-temp. (high-Tc) supercond. in layered copper oxides, many researchers have searched for similar behavior in other layered metal oxides involving 3d transition metals, such as cobalt and nickel.  Such attempts have so far failed, with the result that the copper oxide layer is thought to be essential for supercond.  Here, the authors report that NaxCoO2·yH2O (x ≈ 0.35, yr ≈ 1.3) is a superconductor with a Tc of about 5 K.  This compd. consists of two-dimensional CoO2 layers sepd. by a thick insulating layer of Na+ ions and H2O mols.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Rechargeable Li batteries have risen to prominence as key devices for green and sustainable energy development.  Mn- and Fe-based cathode materials, such as LiMn2O4 and LiFePO4, are used in large-scale batteries for elec. vehicles.  Mn and Fe are abundant elements in the Earth's crust, but Li is not.  In contrast to Li, Na is an attractive charge carrier from elemental abundance.  Recently, some layered materials, where Na can be electrochem. and reversibly extd./inserted, have been reported.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Layered NaNi0.5Mn0.5O2 (space group: R3‾m), having an O3-type (α-NaFeO2 type) structure according to the Delmas' notation, is prepd. by a solid-state method.  The electrochem. reactivity of NaNi0.5Mn0.5O2 is examd. in an aprotic sodium cell at room temp.  The NaNi0.5Mn0.5O2 electrodes can deliver ca. 105-125 mAh g-1 at rates of 240-4.8 mA g-1 in the voltage range of 2.2-3.8 V and show 75% of the initial reversible capacity after 50 charge/discharge cycling tests.  In the voltage range of 2.2-4.5 V, a higher reversible capacity of 185 mAh g-1 is achieved; however, its reversibility is insufficient because of the significant expansion of interslab space by charging to 4.5 V vs. sodium.  The reversibility is improved by adding fluoroethylene carbonate into the electrolyte soln.  The structural transition mechanism of Na1-xNi0.5Mn0.5O2 is also examd. by an ex situ X-ray diffraction method combined with X-ray absorption spectroscopy (XAS).  The staking sequence of the [Ni0.5Mn0.5]O2 slabs changes progressively as sodium ions are extd. from the crystal lattice.  It is obsd. that the original O3 phase transforms into the O'3, P3, P'3, and P3'' phases during sodium extn.  XAS measurement proves that NaNi0.5Mn0.5O2 consists of divalent nickel and tetravalent manganese ions.  As sodium ions are extd. from the oxide to form Na1-xNi0.5Mn0.5O2, nickel ions are oxidized to the trivalent state, while the manganese ions are electrochem. inactive as the tetravalent state.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrrz7r6m5EEZLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljt_Qt3lqLyzw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38XnsVKkurk%253D&amp;md5=d6d57b28847976301d6d8e4e186cf438</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref11"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref11" class="rightTabRefNumLink">11</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit11" data-doi="10.1016/j.electacta.2013.09.098"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liao, X.-Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">He, Y.-S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ma, Z.-F.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Electrochemical properties of P2-Na<sub>2/3</sub>[Ni<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>2/3</sub>]O<sub>2</sub> cathode material for sodium ion batteries when cycled in different voltage ranges</span>. <i>Electrochim.         </div><div class="casAbstract">P2-type Na2/3[Ni1/3Mn2/3]O2 cathode material has been synthesized via spray drying method and a two-step solid state process.  Electrochem. behavior of the prepd. material as cathode material for sodium-ion battery was investigated in different charge-discharge voltage ranges.  The results indicated that the cycling performance of the P2-Na2/3[Ni1/3Mn2/3]O2 cathode greatly depended on the voltage window.  The material showed excellent reversibility between 2.0 V and 4.0 V with reversible capacity of 86 mA-h/g (0.1C) and 77 mA-h/g (1C).  X-ray diffraction analyses indicated that crystal structure of the P2-type Na2/3[Ni1/3Mn2/3]O2 could be well maintained after long-term cycling in 2.0-4.0 V.  When the upper limiting voltage was increased to 4.5 V, the crystal structure of P2-Na2/3[Ni1/3Mn2/3]O2 was irreversibly damaged due to over-extn. of Na+ in 4.0-4.5 V.  On the other hand, when the cycling voltage range was between 1.6 V and 3.8 V, the discharge capacities increased to 135 mA-h/g (0.1C) and 108 mA-h/g (1C), resp.  However, the cycling stability in 1.6-3.8 V was not as excellent as that in 2.0-4.0 V.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Based on low-cost and rich resources, sodium-ion batteries have been regarded as a promising candidate for next-generation energy storage batteries in the large-scale energy applications of renewable energy and smart grids.  However, there are some crit. drawbacks limiting its application, such as safety and stability problems.  In this work, a stable sym. sodium-ion battery based on the bipolar, active O3-type material, Na0.8Ni0.4Ti0.6O2, is developed.  This bipolar material shows a typical O3-type layered structure, contg. two electrochem. active transition metals with redox couples of Ni4+/Ni2+ and Ti4+/Ti3+, resp.  This Na0.8Ni0.4Ti0.6O2-based sym. cell exhibits a high av. voltage of 2.8 V, a reversible discharge capacity of 85 mA h g-1, 75% capacity retention after 150 cycles and good rate capability.  This full sym. cell will greatly contribute to the development of room-temp. sodium-ion batteries with a view towards safety, low cost and long life, and it will stimulate further research on sym. cells using the same active materials as both cathode and anode.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqmF3B7AwSKZ7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liUbMFcTUd3Xw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXitlamsLw%253D&amp;md5=ea808bbce1fe8dbd563aeb8d25a9e1e7</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref18"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref18" class="rightTabRefNumLink">18</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit18"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Carmichael, R.         </div><div class="casAbstract">With the shortage of fossil energy and global climate change, renewable energy sources offer the most promising solns. to these challenges.  However, the intermittence of energy sources such as solar and wind requires sustainable energy storage technologies.  Nowadays, electrochem. energy storage technologies and applications have attracted much attention, including portable elec. devices, elec. vehicles and smart power grids.  Lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) have been widely applied in these areas because of their safety, portability and high energy d.  Nevertheless, the resources of lithium are limited and the vast consumption of lithium has pushed up the price of lithium compds., urging people to search for resourceful materials.  Sodium can be a promising alternative due to its high abundance and low cost.  Furthermore, titanium-based materials have become a hot spot for anode materials in sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), with their advantages of high safety and structural stability.  In this review, we summarize the recent advances in Ti-based anode materials for SIB applications.  We highlight the design and engineering of Ti-based nanoarchitectures, esp. emphasizing the effective enhancement in performance and the related sodium storage mechanism.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpa4N56ebVXIbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljSZPIUh2l-Fw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XhtVynt73M&amp;md5=40308f2ab8b2beb4f73c5fe67992c973</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref20"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref20" class="rightTabRefNumLink">20</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit20" data-doi="10.1016/j.nima.2011.11.070"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hoelzel, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Senyshyn, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Juenke, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Boysen, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Schmahl, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fuess, H.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">High-resolution neutron powder diffractometer SPODI at research reactor FRM II</span>. <i>Nucl.         </div><div class="casAbstract">SPODI is a high-resoln. thermal neutron diffractometer at the research reactor Heinz Maier-Leibnitz (FRM II) esp. dedicated to structural studies of complex systems.  Unique features like a very large monochromator take-off angle of 155° and a 5 m monochromator-sample distance in its std. configuration achieve both high-resoln. and a good profile shape for a broad scattering angle range.  Two dimensional data are collected by an array of 80 vertical position sensitive 3He detectors.  SPODI is well suited for studies of complex structural and magnetic order and disorder phenomena at non-ambient conditions.  In addn. to std. sample environment facilities (cryostats, furnaces, magnet) specific devices (rotatable load frame, cell for elec. fields, multichannel potentiostat) were developed.  Thus the characterization of functional materials at in-operando conditions can be achieved.  In this contribution the details of the design and present performance of the instrument are reported along with its specifications.  A new concept for data redn. using a 2θ dependent variable height for the intensity integration along the Debye-Scherrer lines is introduced.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqVM-cL5upTcLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljSZPIUh2l-Fw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38XktFemsg%253D%253D&amp;md5=f67a26cfea5ffb605e4b683e7468ba11</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref21"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref21" class="rightTabRefNumLink">21</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit21" data-doi="10.4028/www.scientific.net/msf.378-381.118"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Roisnel, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Rodríquez-Carvajal, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">WinPLOTR: A Windows Tool for Powder Diffraction Pattern Analysis</span>. <i>Mater.     </div><div class="casAbstract">Theor. x-ray absorption fine structure (XAFS) stds. were developed for arbitrary pairs of atoms throughout the periodic table (Z ≤94).  These std. XAFS spectra were obtained from ab initio single-scattering XAFS calcns., using an automated code, FEFF, which takes into account the most important features in current theories: (1) an exact treatment of curved-wave effects; (2) approx. mol. potentials derived from relativistic atoms; (3) a complex, energy-dependent self-energy; and (4) a well defined energy ref.  FEFF also yields tables of XAFS phases and amplitudes as well as mean-free paths.  Sample results are presented and compared with exptl. results and with earlier work.  These theor. stds. are competitive with exptl. stds., permitting XAFS anal. at lower wave nos. and yielding distance detns. typically better than 0.02 Å and coordination nos. typically better than 20%.     </div><div class="casAbstract">An investigation of the Na-Co-O system allows the isolation of 4 new bronze type phases with the formula NaxCoO2 (x ≤ 1).  The structure consists of sheets of octahedra (CoO2)n between which are inserted the Na ions.  Their structures differ from one another by the stacking sequence of the O layers, and by the distortions introduced as a result of the ordering of vacancies among the Na layers.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The surface compn. and chem. environment of LiCoO2, hexagonal LiNiO2, cubic LiNiO2, and the mixed transition metal oxide, LiNi0.5Co0.5O2, have been detd. by Auger electron and x-ray photoelectron spectroscopies.  Although the LiCoO2 surface properties can easily be extrapolated from bulk compn., the Ni-contg. materials are less amenable to this procedure.  Their surface concn. tends to be depleted in Li relative to that of the bulk and shows an atypical chem. environment for the constituent elements.  The Ni 2p XPS photoemission suggests a near NiO-like selvedge through the XPS binding energies and satellite structure which are essentially identical to that of NiO - the spectrum appears fairly insensitive to Li concn.  Although there is little evidence for higher binding energy Ni3+ species or for an electron poor Ni2.δ+-derived band structure in the XPS, the lattice O is electron-rich and yields among the lowest binding energies reported for a transition metal oxide.  The Ni-contg. Li oxide selvedge is thus not simply NiO and the surface Li cations have a measurable effect on the electronic structure even in their more highly depleted levels.  This is explained in the context of the charge-transfer model of the oxide band structure.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGob-8tvLat_Z7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhBvQuT3lbD1Q"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD2sXitV2ku74%253D&amp;md5=b62674f809db287789c048a928e52349</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref29"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref29" class="rightTabRefNumLink">29</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit29" data-doi="10.1103/physrevb.56.1290"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Saitoh, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Mizokawa, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fujimori, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Abbate, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Takeda, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Takano, M.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Electronic structure and magnetic states in La<sub>1–<i>x</i></sub>Sr<sub><i>x</i></sub>CoO<sub>3</sub> studied by photoemission and x-ray-absorption spectroscopy</span>. <i>Phys. The exptl. results are compared with model cluster calcns.  The CoO band gap is of an intermediate character, between Mott-Hubbard-like and charge-transfer-like.  The first ionization state of CoO is therefore of strongly mmixed Co 3d and O 2p character.  Its local symmetry corresponds to 3T1g, similar to an intermediate-spin Co3+ state.                         rightTab-cas_cit44R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">44</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Understanding the Structural Evolution and Redox Mechanism of a NaFeO2-NaCoO2 Solid Solution for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kubota, Kei; Asari, Takuya; Yoshida, Hiroaki; Yaabuuchi, Naoaki; Shiiba, Hiromasa; Nakayama, Masanobu; Komaba, Shinichi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Functional Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">26</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">33</span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-ion batteries have become promising candidates for large-scale energy-storage systems because of the abundant Na resources and they have attracted considerable academic interest because of their unique behavior, such as their electrochem. activity for the Fe3+/Fe4+ redox couple.  The high-rate performance derived from the low Lewis-acidity of the Na+ ions is another advantage of Na-ion batteries and has been demonstrated in NaFe1/2Co1/2O2 solns.  Here, a solid soln. of NaFeO2-NaCoO2 is synthesized and the mechanisms behind their excellent electrochem. performance are studied in comparison to those of their resp. end-members.  The combined anal. of operando X-ray diffraction, ex situ X-ray absorption spectroscopy, and d. functional theory (DFT) calcns. for Na1-xFe1/2Co1/2O2 reveals that the O3-type phase transforms into a P3-type phase coupled with Na+/vacancy ordering, which has not been obsd. in O3-type NaFeO2.  The substitution of Co for Fe stabilizes the P3-type phase formed by sodium extn. and could suppress the irreversible structural change that is usually obsd. in O3-type NaFeO2, resulting in a better cycle retention and higher rate performance.  Although no ordering of the transition metal ions is seen in the neutron diffraction expts., as supported by Monte-Carlo simulations, the formation of a superlattice originating from the Na+/vacancy ordering is found by synchrotron X-ray diffraction for Na0.5Fe1/2Co1/2O2, which may involve a potential step in the charge/discharge profiles.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrIOOIWAWpvkLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhLd7wCfbgq-w"> &gt;&gt;                         "><div class="casContent">There is no corresponding record for this reference.</div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref46"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref46" class="rightTabRefNumLink">46</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit46" data-doi="10.1016/j.elecom.2013.05.012"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yoshida, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yabuuchi, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Komaba, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">NaFe<sub>0.5</sub>Co<sub>0.5</sub>O<sub>2</sub> as High Energy and Power Positive Electrode for Na-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Electrochem. Commun.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2013</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>34</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">60</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">63</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1016/j.elecom.2013.05.012</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit46R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">46</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">NaFe0.5Co0.5O2 as high energy and power positive electrode for Na-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yoshida, Hiroaki; Yabuuchi, Naoaki; Komaba, Shinichi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Electrochemistry Communications</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">34</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">60-63</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ECCMF9</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1388-2481</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">O3-type NaFeO2 is a promising candidate as pos. electrode materials for rechargeable Na batteries.  However, its reversible range of sodium extn. is relatively narrow (x = ca. 0.4 in Na1-xFeO2) because of the irreversible structural change, presumably assocd. with the iron migration into the adjacent tetrahedral sites in Na layers as we recently reported.  Herein, we report that cobalt-substituted NaFeO2 demonstrates excellent electrode performance in a non-aq. Na cell at room temp.  NaFe0.5Co0.5O2 delivers approx. 160 mAh g-1 of reversible capacity with relatively good capacity retention and excellent rate-capability in a voltage range of 2.5-4.0 V, resulting in reversible formation of Na0.3Fe0.5Co0.5O2.  The partial substitution of metal element for Fe in O3-type NaFeO2-based materials is proved to be the important strategy to suppress the irreversible phase transition, and thus improving the reversibility of sodium removal/insertion as the electrode materials.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrNK15FzKNlgrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhLd7wCfbgq-w"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXhtlyjtbfO&amp;md5=a4e8ef4b34c630431cd1b25d6438e076</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref47"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref47" class="rightTabRefNumLink">47</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit47" data-doi="10.1007/bf00658014"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Maazaz, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Delmas, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hagenmuller, P.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">A study of the Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>TiO<sub>2</sub> system by electrochemical deintercalation</span>. <i>J. Incl. Phenom.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">1983</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>1</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">45</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">51</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1007/bf00658014</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit47R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">47</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A study of the sodium titanium oxide (NaxTiO2) system by electrochemical deintercalation</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Maazaz, A.; Delmas, C.; Hagenmuller, P.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Inclusion Phenomena</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">1983</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">1</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">45-51</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JOIPDF</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0167-7861</span>.
    </div><div class="casAbstract">The electrochem. deintercalation from the sheet oxide NaTiO2 leads to new NaxTiO2 materials.  The deintercalation is reversible over the range 0.70 .ltorsim. x ≤ 1, whereas irreversible modification of the host structure occur by overvoltage (x .ltorsim. 0.70).  This behavior, which can be explained by migration of some Ti ions from the slabs to the intersheet space, may be considered as a 2-dimensional → 3-dimensional structural transition.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGokeg4GiB6gBrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhLd7wCfbgq-w"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADyaL3sXls1Gltbc%253D&amp;md5=bd2b4401a87d4bbf694524aba89fbb5b</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref48"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref48" class="rightTabRefNumLink">48</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit48" data-doi="10.1039/c5cp06179b"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lu, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Song, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Pan, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cheng, Y.-T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Voltage Hysteresis of Lithium Ion Batteries Caused by Mechanical Stress</span>. <i>Phys. Chem. Chem. Phys.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2016</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>18</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">4721</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">4727</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1039/c5cp06179b</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord empty" id="
                            rightTab-cas_R
                        "><div class="casContent">There is no corresponding record for this reference.</div></div></div></div></div></li></ol></li><li id="pane-m3" aria-labelledby="pane-m3" class="tab__pane tab__pane-suppInfo"><h2 class="supp-info-label">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article_supporting-info" id="rightTab-notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="rightTab-_i23">Supporting Information</h2></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge on the <a href="/" class="ext-link">ACS Publications website</a> at DOI: <a href="/doi/abs/10.1021/acsami.8b11609" class="ext-link">10.1021/acsami.8b11609</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Structural parameters of O3- and P3-phases, cycling performance, comparison of Na content from Rietveld refinement and GCPL measurements, EXAFS fitting results, and comparison of Co valence states from XAS edge and GCPL measurements (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b11609/suppl_file/am8b11609_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.8b11609/suppl_file/am8b11609_si_001.pdf">am8b11609_si_001.pdf (1.01 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div><div id="rightTab-figshare-widget"></div><br><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzoneRight" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzoneRight"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="b84e6b6c-b4d0-4427-b286-3cf14d52d013" class="mb-4">

(function(){
	
	/*Event Tracking for RIS menu dropdown - Article pages*/

	$('.cit-download-dropdown_button').on('click',function(e)
	{	
		e.preventDefault();
	    gtag('event', 'Export', { 'event_category': 'Citation', 'event_label':'Main'});

	});


	$('.cit-download-dropdown_content ul li').on('click',function(e)
	{	
	//	e.preventDefault();
	    var idx = $(this).index();
	    
	    var label = '';
	    if (idx === 0 ) {
	    	label = 'Citation';
	    } else if (idx === 1 ) {
	    	label = 'Abstract';
	    } else if (idx === 2 ) {
	    	label = 'Reference';
	    } else if (idx === 3 ) {
	    	label = 'More Options';
	    }
	   
	   gtag('event', 'Export', { 'event_category': 'Citation', 'event_label':label});
	});

	$('.header_contnav-next a').on('click', function(e){
        e.preventDefault();
        gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Right Arrow'});
	}); 

 	$('.header_contnav-pre a').on('click', function(e){
	    e.preventDefault();
	     gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Left Arrow'});
	}); 

	$('.share_button').on('click', function(e){
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Share'});
	});

	$("#pane-m1con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Figures'});
	});

	$("#pane-m2con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
	    gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'References'});
	});

	$("#pane-m3con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Supporting Info'});
	});

	$('.tab__close').on('click', function(){
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Close'});
	}); 



})();

</script>

<script nonce="90f6eacd59b8775c-SJC">
    document.addEventListener("DOMContentLoaded", function(){
    // Function to get a Human readable formatted date
    function getFormattedDateForMaintenance() {
        let today = new Date();
        let dd = String(today.getDate()).padStart(2, '0');
        //January is 0
        let mm = String(today.getMonth() + 1).padStart(2, '0'); 
        let yyyy = today.getFullYear();
        
        let daysOfWeek = ['Sunday', 'Monday', 'Tuesday', 'Wednesday', 'Thursday', 'Friday', 'Saturday'];
        let monthsOfYear = ['January', 'February', 'March', 'April', 'May', 'June', 'July', 'August', 'September', 'October', 'November', 'December'];

        let dayOfWeek = daysOfWeek[today.getDay()];
        let monthOfYear = monthsOfYear[today.getMonth()];

        return dayOfWeek + ', ' + monthOfYear + ' ' + dd + ', ' + yyyy;
    }
    
    // Insert the date into the .system-message-date element
    document.querySelector('.system-message-date').innerText = getFormattedDateForMaintenance();
});
</script>

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="90f6eacd59b8775c-SJC">

	/*

var infoCentralCapture = (function() {

|\-|\/)|ibro|idea|ig01|ikom|im1k|inno|ipaq|iris|ja(t|v)a|jbro|jemu|jigs|kddi|keji|kgt( |\/)|klon|kpt |kwc\-|kyo(c|k)|le(no|xi)|lg( g|\/(k|l|u)|50|54|\-[a-w])|libw|lynx|m1\-w|m3ga|m50\/|ma(te|ui|xo)|mc(01|21|ca)|m\-cr|me(rc|ri)|mi(o8|oa|ts)|mmef|mo(01|02|bi|de|do|t(\-| |o|v)|zz)|mt(50|p1|v )|mwbp|mywa|n10[0-2]|n20[2-3]|n30(0|2)|n50(0|2|5)|n7(0(0|1)|10)|ne((c|m)\-|on|tf|wf|wg|wt)|nok(6|i)|nzph|o2im|op(ti|wv)|oran|owg1|p800|pan(a|d|t)|pdxg|pg(13|\-([1-8]|c))|phil|pire|pl(ay|uc)|pn\-2|po(ck|rt|se)|prox|psio|pt\-g|qa\-a|qc(07|12|21|32|60|\-[2-7]|i\-)|qtek|r380|r600|raks|rim9|ro(ve|zo)|s55\/|sa(ge|ma|mm|ms|ny|va)|sc(01|h\-|oo|p\-)|sdk\/|se(c(\-|0|1)|47|mc|nd|ri)|sgh\-|shar|sie(\-|m)|sk\-0|sl(45|id)|sm(al|ar|b3|it|t5)|so(ft|ny)|sp(01|h\-|v\-|v )|sy(01|mb)|t2(18|50)|t6(00|10|18)|ta(gt|lk)|tcl\-|tdg\-|tel(i|m)|tim\-|t\-mo|to(pl|sh)|ts(70|m\-|m3|m5)|tx\-9|up(\.b|g1|si)|utst|v400|v750|veri|vi(rg|te)|vk(40|5[0-3]|\-v)|vm40|voda|vulc|vx(52|53|60|61|70|80|81|83|85|98)|w3c(\-| )|webc|whit|wi(g |nc|nw)|wmlb|wonu|x700|yas\-|your|zeto|zte\-/i.test(a.substr(0,4))) check = true;})(navigator.userAgent||navigator.vendor||window.opera);
              return check;
        };

    var _isMobile = window.mobilecheck();
    if (_isMobile) {
        $('#gdpr-message-modal').addClass('mobile');
    }

    var emailValid = "",
        time = 3000,
        btnStatus = true,
        errorStatus = false,
        errormessage = "<p class='gdpr_err'><b>Please Enter a valid email address<b><p>";

    function init() {
        setTimeout(function() {
            var cookieExist = getCookie("gdpr");
            if (cookieExist) {
                //console.log("gdpr cookie exist");
                return false;
            } else {
                //console.log("gdpr cookie does not exist")
                gdprMessage();
            }
            //Events
            $('#closebtn').on("click", hideForm);

            //Handle submit
            $('#gdpr-message-btn').on("click", function(event) {
                handleClick();
            });

            // //Hide if clicked outside div - TO DO stop handling click after submit
            // $(document).click(function(event) {
            // 	event.stopPropagation();
            //   console.log(event.target);
            //   if (!$(event.target).closest("#gdpr-message-content").length) {
            //     $("body").find("#gdpr-message-modal").hide();
            //     $(this).off();
            //   }
            // });
            $('#gdpr-con-btn').click(function() {
                setCookie("gdpr", "true");
                hideForm();
            });
        }, time);
    }

    //Show the message
    function gdprMessage() {
        //console.log("Show GDPR Message");
        var tMessage, emailInput, messageButton, ewrap;

        $('#gdpr-message-modal').show();
    }

    function handleClick() {
        //console.log($("#gdpr-input").val());
        emailValid = validateEmail($("#gdpr-input").val());
        if (emailValid) {
            //Submit the form
            //Hide form
            if ($('.gdpr_err')) {
                $('.gdpr_err').hide();
            }

            $("#gdpr-message-content")
                //If successful then...
            setCookie("gdpr", "true");
            //console.log("emailValid " + emailValid + " submiting form");
        } else {
            //Show error message
            gdprErrorMessage();
            //console.log("emailValid " + emailValid);
        }
    }

    function gdprErrorMessage() {
        if (errorStatus) {
            return false;
        } else {
            errorStatus = true;
            $("#gdpr-message-content").append(errormessage);
        }
    }

    function validateEmail(email) {
        var re = /^(([^<>()\[\]\\.,;:\s@"]+(\.[^<>()\[\]\\.,;:\s@"]+)*)|(".+"))@((\[[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\])|(([a-zA-Z\-0-9]+\.)+[a-zA-Z]{2,}))$/;
        return re.test(String(email).toLowerCase());
    }

    function setCookie(name, value) {
        var expires = "";
        expires = "; expires=Fri, 31 Dec 9999 23:59:59 GMT";
        document.cookie = name + "=" + (value || "") + expires + "; path=/";
    }

    function getCookie(name) {
        var match = document.cookie.match(new RegExp(name + '=([^;]+)'));
        if (match) return match[1];
    }

    function hideForm() { //And set cookie
        $('#gdpr-message-modal').hide();
        setCookie("gdpr", "false");
        gtag('event', 'GDPR Modal', { 'event_category': 'Pubs GDPR', 'event_label':'GDPR Dismissal'});
    }

    return {
        init: init
    }
})();

$(document).ready(infoCentralCapture.init);
</script>


                    
                        



        
        <script nonce="90f6eacd59b8775c-SJC">
var currentJournal = "ACS Applied Materials &amp;amp; Interfaces";
$(".citationLinks > a").click(function(e){
    if($(this).text() === "ACS Full Text ") {
        gtag('event', 'ACS Full Text', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "Crossref") {
        gtag('event', 'Crossref', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "CAS") {
        gtag('event', 'CAS', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "PubMed") {
        gtag('event', 'PubMed', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "Google Scholar") {
        gtag('event', 'Google Scholar', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).hasClass("issue-item_open-url") === true) {
        gtag('event', 'OpenURL', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
});
</script>

    console.log("ACSPubs2 start");

            if ( status == 'success' ) {
                $.each(data, function(i, field){
                    cookie=cookie.concat(i+' = '+field+" - ");

                    cookie=status;

            //TODO send cookie val to the controller for logging

    })
    .fail(function(xx, status, err){
